# 304\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Claudius D iii: Benedictine Rule}\n
C13a1\n
478 155 N\n
{=Wintney, Hants=}\n
{=Sample tagged - fols. 52r-54v, 55r-v=}\n
{=Script - large early Gothic book hand resembling those used for church\n
books=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Insular <r> is used beside usual Textura short <r> and 2-shaped\n
<r> after rightward facing bows. Use of insular <r> seems sometimes to\n
have caused confusion with long <s>. The scribe also uses insular <g>\n
(beside Caroline <g>. Differentiation of their funcions is not\n
regularly maintained, and the scribe has a flat-topped Caroline <g>,\n
as well as a more usual-shaped one, that may be some attempt at a\n
compromise between the two figurae. The shape of <y> is rather\n
variable, though is mostly kept distinct from wynn and is often dotted=}\n
{=The Rule is in alternate Latin and an English translation. The\n
English is on fols. 52r-54v, 55r-v, 58r-60r, 61r-v, 62v-67v, 68v-82r,\n
83v-92r, 93v-95r, 96v-103v, 104v-112v, 113v-115v, 116v-118v,\n
119v-126r, 127r-v, 128v-130r, 131r-138r. For the Latin, see\n
Schroer (1888) where it is printed on alternate pages parallel\n
to the English. The English is evidently based closely on an\n
Old English version and is much more Old English than Middle English.\n
Only a short sample has therefore been transcribed and tagged here in\n
order to illustrate some interesting show-throughs of what may be\n
contemporary (ca 1200) Hampshire English=}\n
{~f52ra}\n
{)\'*HER ONgYNd URES HALIES \\ FaeDER REgOLAn SanCteS *BENEDICTes\')}\n
{\\}\n
{=The title appears to be in a different hand from the main hand of\n
English. Both being very formal book hands, it is difficult to be sure\n
that they are different, but the shape of insular g in each is\n
distinct. The title also appears to be in a less archaic language too -\n
using two strong adjectives in a row=}\n
$hear/v-imp_*gE-HUR $ge-/xp-v-imp_*gE-\n
{=Two-line coloured decorated initial insular g, with decorative\n
{flourishes descending two further lines down in the left margin=}\n

$ateli:c/aj-sup_ATE+LUC+EST $-ly/xs-aj-sup_+LUC+EST $-est/xs-sup-aj_+EST\n
{=This seems to be a mix up of two different possible OE\n
constructions. The original OE does not have the word \'mid\' and is simply:\n
"the third kind of nuns is most terrible of all". With \'mid\' added we\n
might expect \'mid ealle\' or \'mid eallum\' (mid takes dat, inst, or acc):\n
"the third kind of nuns is altogether most terrible". I have chosen to\n
tag this construction taking MID-EALRE as a "withal, altogther"\n
construction in spite of the unexpected genitive pl form of "all", otherwise the\n
\'mid\' would be redundant and the tag would interpret EALRE as an alder-\n
prefix to the superlative=}\n

$with{w}/pr_wITH\n
{=This is taken in both MED (cited as yICH) and OED (cited as yIEH) to\n
be a unique transitive usage of the verb Ye:on - "the Lord cause you\n
to thrive". The expected sense is "The Lord is (or be) with thee". The\n
letter before final H is certainly not E and may be read as either C\n
or T in this scribe\'s hand. The first letter lacks the usual ascender\n
of this scribe\'s thorn and is open at the top. Although this scribe\n
elsewhere uses W not wynn I think this is nevertheless a wynn (perhaps\n
taken over from the exemplar) and that the word is "with". The lack\n
of copular verb makes the English here directly parallel to the Latin\n
- \'Dominus tecum\'. Neither yICH nor yIEH are plausible forms for the\n
present subjunctive of Ye:on in any case=}\n

{=Sample tagged: fols. 4r-48r=}\n
{=Script - Protogothic book hand with some cursive tendencies and\n
elements of contemporary document hand=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Scribe A has an elaborate form of punctus elevatus which is\n
identical in shape to the usual symbol for punctus interrogativus,\n
i.e. it has a distinct hook on the top stroke instead of just a\n
comma-shaped curve. Scribe A uses the same symbol for both purposes.\n
Dobson (1972) prints punctus elevatus for this symbol whatever the\n
context. I transcribe as .\' where there is no question suggested by the\n
syntax. Where there is, I transcribe ? taking into account the use of\n
? in the other versions of Ancrene Riwle=}\n
{~f4r~}\n
{=Corrections to the text, by both Scribe B and Scribe D, (and other\n
later correctors) are extensive and are clearly and amply dealt with\n
in Dobson (1972). For the sake of simplicity, therefore, additions and\n
corrections are not noted here unless they provide missing words\n
necessary for understanding the text. Then they are noted using my\n
usual conventions. Where text by Scribe A has been erased or partially\n
erased by Scribe B or D and is still legible or deducible, Scribe A\'s\n
text is restored with the partially visible letters in square\n
brackets. This follows Dobson\'s (1972) practice, which see for\n
details. For substantial contributions by Scribe B, see separate\n
{tagged text - no. 275. For Scribe D\'s contributions see no. 1700=}\n
{=Decorated and coloured initial *R taking up four lines\' depth=}\n

GRAMMATICUM \\ *RECTUM GEOMETRICUM . RECTUM THEOLO\\GICUM . & SUNT DIFFERENCIE\n
TOTIDEM RE\\GULARUm . *DERECTO THEOLOGICO SERMO NOBIS EST . \\ *CUIUS DUE SUNT\n
REGuLE . VNA CIRCA *CORDIS DIRec\\CiOnEM .\' ALTerA UERSATUR CircA EXTerIORum\n
RecTIFICACiOnEM . \\ *RECTI DILIGUNT TE(}\n

{=Scribe A started to write the word but omitted the I and then\n
underlined the whole for removal repeating it correctly afterwards.\n
Half the original S and most of the E are now lost in what I assume to\n
be a subsequent hole in the parchment. Dobson considers the hole to have\n
been present at the time of Scribe A\'s writing. But if it was it must\n
have enlarged since to explain the partial loss of S and E. Scribe A\n
would have no reason to write half letters, but would have continued\n
the word after the hole as he does elsewhere. The reason for the\n
deletion was evidently the misspelling not the hole=}\n

$unmae:rehae:lu?/n<pr_HUN+MERE+HEALE $un-/xp-n_HUN+ $hae:lu/n<pr-k_+HEALE\n
{"inglorious?, miserable?"}\n
{=Dobson (1972) reads HIMMERE, but the minims can be plausibly\n
split as above and it would then be easy to adopt the reading\n
suggested in MED of unmae:re. But there is likely to be some\n
corruption as Corpus also has HIMMERE or HUNMERE HEILE while Nero\n
and Titus leave it out. Neither the Corpus scribe nor the Cleopatra\n
scribe have HUN- for UN- elsewhere, though excresent H- forms are\n
known in other EME texts and in the SWML especially in Digby 86.\n
It could also possibly be read as HUNIUERE for UNFERE meaning "sick",\n
"diseased", "harmful" (cf. UNIFELE, UNIdOR, UNIFOH, etc. Millett\n
(2005) emends the Corpus text to HINENE HULEN on the basis that the\n
scriptural passage analysed here should include the "tabernacula\n
pastorum". This may very well be a satisfactory textual emendation\n
and what was intended by the author. It does not help us to work out\n
what the Cleopatra scribe thought he was writing here=}\n
{.}\n

$god/nOd_GODx\n
{=Diagonal stroke going up from left to right after D (not crossing it\n
at all) apparently by Scribe A himself. Dobson (1972) suggests that it\n
indicates that the word should be read as "god" not "good" and in effect\n
that the D should be doubled. I think this unconvincing, since elsewhere\n
Scribe A does not differentiate "god" from "good" except sometimes by\n
doubling the O in "good", and here the context gives ample indication\n
of the required reading. Scribe A does not use double D in "god"\n
anywhere in this sample. If the stroke is by Scribe A, its implication\n
is not clear=}\n

# 275\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Cleopatra C.vi, hand B\'s corrections}\n
C13a2\n
352 275 N\n
{=Ludlow, S Salop=}\n
{=Sample represents all the continuous English and whole word\n
corrections in this hand. See mainly fols. 4r-26v line 5,\n
124v-130v, 191r-198v. Also fols. 58r, 80r, 183r=}\n
{=Script - Formal C13 document hand (M.B. Parkes, pers. com.)=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The following text consists of Scribe B\'s marginal and interlinear\n
corrections to the text of Ancrene Riwle written by Scribe A. In most\n
cases these corrections bring Scribe A\'s original text, version C,\n
into line with the revised text found in Cambridge, Corpus Christi\n
College MS 402 (version A, Ancrene Wisse). For this and other reasons,\n
Dobson argues (1972: xciii-cxl, esp. xcvi) that Scribe B of BL Cotton\n
Cleopatra C.vi was the author of Ancrene Riwle/Wisse, whom he further\n
tentatively identifies with "Brianus de Lingan Canonicus secularis\n
monesterii de wigmore" via interpretation of the anagram at the end\n
of the text (Dobson 1976: 312-68). Bella Millet (1992) has thrown\n
doubt on the detail of Dobson\'s conclusions and she argues strongly for\n
a Dominican author for Ancrene Riwle/Wisse. The Dominican house that\n
would most closely fit with the linguistic facts is Shrewsbury. It is\n
true that a Dominican Friar based in Shrewsbury at the time of writing\n
Ancrene Riwle would not need to have originated from there, but\n
linguistically there is nothing to prevent Scribe B\'s language from\n
being fitted there. If one accepts the placings of the language of the\n
Titus Ancrene Riwle and of the Titus Wooing of our Lord, the forms in\n
those texts give a more northerly confirmation of some of Scribe B\'s\n
forms at this early date. The somewhat contrasting Shrewsbury\n
material in LALME is from 200 years later. The details of the positioning of\n
Scribe B\'s corrections and revisions within the main text may be found\n
in Dobson (1972) and are not repeated here. I have not attempted to\n
transcribe bits of words added or substituted by Scribe B, only whole\n
words and longer stretches of text. These are given the grammatical\n
tags suitable to their position in the surrounding text, which may\n
be found in Dobson (1972). Although the text here is thus\n
discontinuous, line breaks and folio references are indicated in the\n
usual way. Braces with no back slash indicate textual discontinuity\n
but no line break=}\n
{~f4r~}\n

$lady/nG_LEAFDI $/Gn_0\n
$5/qc_FIF\n
$joy/npl<pr_GOI+E*S $/pln<pr_+E*S\n
{=These last five words in a rather cursive version of scribe B\'s hand.\n
Dobson reads GOIES with no comment on the form of the final letter.\n
The top half begins like a \'Z\' but the bottom half is very florid - \n
ore like some form of yogh in some scripts, but not in Scribe B\'s.\n
Scribe B\'s usual \'S\' in most contexts is the normal basic long \'S\'.\n
But he does use a capital \'S\' in *SULLI on fol. 21r. This marginal\n
addition is much neater than the one here, but the bottom of the \'S\'\n
of *SULLI is very like the bottom of the final letter of GOIES.\n
Althougth the first lobe seems to bend the wrong way, I am inclined\n
to accept Dobson\'s reading and assume that this is a carelessly\n
formed version of scribe B\'s capital \'S\' although note that Dobson\n
does not capitalise it here=} \n
{.}\n
{\\}\n

{=Dobson thinks INOH MEdFUL is a pun on the name Brianus and at the same\n
time an anagram of \'of Linthehum\' (i.e. Lingen, N. Herefords) see\n
